Guardrails ^66YqMXwL

 ^xl5GXFJY

Guardrails in an AI agent context refer to a set of rules, constraints, or guidelines designed to ensure that the AI behaves in a specific way and adheres to certain ethical standards. These guardrails help prevent unintended consequences, ensure privacy and security, maintain fairness, and uphold other important principles. ^WyM4eE7b

Orchestration ^BcLIldv8

AI Agent Orchestration refers to the process of coordinating and managing multiple AI agents or services to achieve a specific goal or set of objectives. In this context, an "AI agent" can be any software system that autonomously performs tasks, learns from data, and makes decisions based on algorithms.  ^qRjOlx8e

These frameworks are essential for creating systems that can learn, adapt, and interact with their environment dynamically.

Distributed Systems: Managing a large number of autonomous entities that need to interact with each other and their environment.

 Robotic Process Automation (RPA): Automating repetitive tasks in business processes using intelligent agents.
 Intelligent Transportation Systems (ITS): Coordinating traffic flow, monitoring vehicle behavior, and optimizing public transportation systems.

 Healthcare: Building intelligent diagnostic tools, personalized treatment plans, and patient care assistants.

 Finance: Automating risk assessment, fraud detection, and investment strategies.

 Smart Cities: Optimizing city operations through the coordination of various intelligent services (e.g., traffic management, public safety, environmental monitoring). ^r6z333Fz

What are agents? ^RpxocqrX

"Agent" can be defined in several ways. Some customers define agents as fully autonomous systems that operate independently over extended periods, using various tools to accomplish complex tasks. Others use the term to describe more prescriptive implementations that follow predefined workflows. At Anthropic, we categorize all these variations as agentic systems, but draw an important architectural distinction between workflows and agents: ^ZLy3lSuL

Workflows ^afwRspxU

Are systems where LLMs and tools are orchestrated through predefined code paths. ^fWv6WsB5

Agents ^R1Hzr7cX

on the other hand, are systems where LLMs dynamically direct their own processes and tool usage, maintaining control over how they accomplish tasks. ^IHLoecjw

Building block: The augmented LLM
(Sub Agents) ^KLDa0wNM

Prompt chaining decomposes a task into a sequence of steps, where each LLM call processes the output of the previous one. You can add programmatic checks (see "gate” in the diagram below) on any intermediate steps to ensure that the process is still on track. ^LqIgJ563

IN ^IpsWg5k2

LLM Call Router ^sbWWc4mx

Out ^UxR8Yg9A

Routing classifies an input and directs it to a specialized followup task. This workflow allows for separation of concerns, and building more specialized prompts. Without this workflow, optimizing for one kind of input can hurt performance on other inputs. ^9N76xL8E

Aggregator ^Wz485XOr

LLMs can sometimes work simultaneously on a task and have their outputs aggregated programmatically. This workflow, parallelization, manifests in two key variations:

    Sectioning: Breaking a task into independent subtasks run in parallel.
    Voting: Running the same task multiple times to get diverse outputs. ^Zzi4ugMl

In the orchestrator-workers workflow, a central LLM dynamically breaks down tasks, delegates them to worker LLMs, and synthesizes their results. ^Ol8FxImd

Solution ^9vDu6fGR

Agents can handle sophisticated tasks, but their implementation is often straightforward. They are typically just LLMs using tools based on environmental feedback in a loop. It is therefore crucial to design toolsets and their documentation clearly and thoughtfully. We expand on best practices for tool development in Appendix 2 ("Prompt Engineering your Tools"). ^RSQ25V58

LLM Call ^0EOtzPuX

Stop ^fmaDwvRH

Environment ^dQQOmjrB

Solution ^9fRd9B74

Regected + Feedback ^ymYxkUJb

Agents ^N4y2Xl51

Human ^OoY4NTto

Success in the LLM space isn't about building the most sophisticated system. It's about building the right system for your needs. Start with simple prompts, optimize them with comprehensive evaluation, and add multi-step agentic systems only when simpler solutions fall short.

When implementing agents, we try to follow three core principles:

    Maintain simplicity in your agent's design.
    Prioritize transparency by explicitly showing the agent’s planning steps.
    Carefully craft your agent-computer interface (ACI) through thorough tool documentation and testing.

Frameworks can help you get started quickly, but don't hesitate to reduce abstraction layers and build with basic components as you move to production. By following these principles, you can create agents that are not only powerful but also reliable, maintainable, and trusted by their users. ^8jc3tBSP

Success ^eXiIDu6a
